### Post by JayRobert on 2010-04-30
Yes, the finale to a game of "water glum."  (The rules are simple: one person is "glum," the others try to make him or her laugh.  They cannot touch the glum person, but other than that, anything goes.  When the "glum" person laughs, the person who made him laugh becomes the "glum" one.)

(It became "water glum" due to the inevitable arguments over whether a person actually laughed or only snickered (sniggered for you brits), smiled, etc. - the "glum" person holds a mouthful of water; if any leaks (or sprays), he is obviously not glum.)

(I was glum one night in LA and there were six or seven professional comedians in the house.)

@jetsam, for your philosophical craving: why do people laugh?

### Post by JayRobert on 2010-05-01
Yes, but they're coming down in price slowly.  In a year or two, they'll be reasonable, I think.  I have a 40GB SSD for everything but /home; that's on three 640 GB opticals in 1.3TB RAID5.

For $4400 you can get a 1TB SSD with r/w of 1.4Gb/s.  Compared to a 1TB Western Digital  for $110, or a 2TB Seagate for $250, both at 6Gb/s, the SSDs aren't quite competitive yet.  (Just think - a 40-disk, 40TB array at 4x the speed (each!) for the price of 1TB of SSD.  Plus an extra large case, of course.  Herh herh herh.)

Are you planning to buy a new 6-core AMD any time soon?

### Post by Zanthir on 2010-10-01
No. I don't like it. It isn't much of a theory. It's more like an observation.
"There was a big bang." 
"What caused it?"
"I dunno."
"Sorry, what is your theory?"
"Oh, the universe began with a big bang."
"Right. And?"
"That's it. That's all I got."
"Oh."
 
On the other hand. I think it is accurate. Very safe. I think I mostly don't like it because it isn't controversial enough. I like FSM theory much more.
 
---------------

why is the earth round?

---

### Post by robsoles on 2010-10-11
Due to it's circumstances. Gravity presses *fairly*  evenly in spheroidal shape around the Earth, this is one of the  circumstances that makes it *appear* round. It is actually slightly oval,  also due to circumstances.

Is 'random' a human idea that doesn't actually exist? (ie., is anything really *random*?)

---

### Post by jroa on 2010-10-25
I think that would depend on whom you asked.  My kids would say that every action they make is random.  They make no decisions on their own.

In school, I was taught that random meant a complete absence of a logical structure.  Therefore, a person can not make a random choice because everything a person does is based on a reason and thinking.

In computers, most random algorithms have a predefined sequence, often based on time, and are therefore are not random.  You can get close to randomness, in the fact that you can have unknown values be generated, but they are not really random because someone had to program the way those values would be selected.

Why is randomness such a fun topic?

---

### Post by robsoles on 2010-10-25
It is only a fun topic approached from certain angles. Many teenagers, particularly intoxicated ones, are very entertained by 'the randomess of it all'. Boffins who want genuinely random results love it while it looks like they might get some but can be caught in moments of despair over it too.


If you 'randomize' on the 'timer' randomly will it be any more random?

---

### Post by jroa on 2010-10-25
It can not be random if it is based on a timer.  It can, however, very closely simulate randomness.

Did I randomly fall upon this thread?

---

### Post by robsoles on 2010-10-25

### Post by bob-linux-user on 2010-12-22
@V1ad (post #3 way back):-
Well, assuming that you are using the old imperial unit "pound" as a unit of mass, not weight, (in the metric system there is no confusion between the unit of mass (kilogram) and force (i.e. weight)(Newton), then I would say that, assuming the pound of gold and pound of feathers are both measured in the presence of a gas (the atmosphere) at the same point and time on the Earth's surface then the gold would actually exert more force (weight) on the surface than the feathers. This is because of the marginal flotation effect of the atmosphere. In a vacuum of course both masses would weigh EXACTLY the same.  It is difficult to visualise. Think instead of weighing 1 kilogram mass of gold and 1 kilogram mass of wood alongside each other in a fishtank. Obviously the gold would exert more force on the bottom of the tank (ie weigh more) and except for some exceptionally dense woods which don't float,the wood would weigh nothing at all. It is the same principle.
Regards
Mr Logic

---

Ya know, that implies that a ship that has sunk to the bottom of it's container weighs more than a ship which is floating atop the water - lol, the wood doesn't 'weigh nothing at all' no matter how well it floats and shaped right the gold could be made to float as well - would it be weightless if I took 1kg of gold and fashioned it into a hull shape of a ship and sat it on the water? NO - it's displacement in the water will make it float but it still weighs 1Kg at the surface of our planet!

### Post by bob-linux-user on 2010-12-22
I think we are getting confused between "mass" and "weight". If I have two masses of 1 kg each they will weigh (ie exert a force) of about 9.80665 Newtons at the Earths surface. My original point is that the 1 kilo (I don't like imperial units for this very reason-the pound is strangely used as a unit of mass and weight) of gold would exert marginally more force than the feathers although you would need to set this up with an extremely fine balance and laboratory conditions to prove it. Both of these 1 kilogram masses would still both be 1 kilogram each if they were on the moon, but weighed (in vacuum) there they would both weigh the same (roughly 1.6 Newtons I think)

---

### Post by robsoles on 2010-12-22
Weight is an effect applied to objects with mass by gravity - if it weighs 1Kg at the surface of our Earth it will weigh less at the surface of the moon if the same weighing equipment is used at both locations. Of course the mass remains the same but as I started this statement: Weight is an effect of gravity on mass.


> **gt3pilot said:**
> Nope...unless you are speaking in terms of relative humidity then air @ 40% RH feels dry...

Why is Linux so cool?

---

### Post by Macskeeball on 2011-06-09
It runs well on old hardware, is very customizable, has technologies like apt & repos which make software discovery, installation, and system-wide updating very easy. All for free, and it's also available for many different architectures. As a project it can't be shutdown because people can always fork if one distro goes under or in a poor direction.
